2:20:982:0:14:72:218:700:12:Times:12:Times:F:Low-Frequency Econometrics
2:21:1008:14:14:72:259:686:12:Times:12:Times:F:Ulrich K. Müller and Mark W. Watson
2:22:1043:14:14:72:234:672:12:Times:12:Times:F:NBER Working Paper No. 21564
2:23:1071:14:14:72:150:658:12:Times:12:Times:F:September 2015

2:25:1104:28:28:306:339:616:12:Times:12:Times:?:ABSTRACT
2:26:1112:28:14:72:540:588:12:Times:12:Times:F:Many questions in economics involve long-run or trend variation and covariation in time series. Yet,
2:27:1212:14:14:72:540:574:12:Times:12:Times:F:time series of typical lengths contain only limited information about this long-run variation. This paper
2:28:1317:14:14:72:540:560:12:Times:12:Times:F:suggests that long-run sample information can be isolated using a small number of low-frequency
2:29:1412:14:14:72:540:546:12:Times:12:Times:F:trigonometric weighted averages, which in turn can be used to conduct inference about long-run variability
2:30:1518:14:14:72:540:532:12:Times:12:Times:F:and covariability. Because the low-frequency weighted averages have large sample normal distributions,
2:31:1620:14:14:72:540:518:12:Times:12:Times:F:large sample valid inference can often be conducted using familiar small sample normal inference
2:32:1716:14:14:72:540:504:12:Times:12:Times:F:procedures.  Moreover, the general approach is applicable for a wide range of persistent stochastic
2:33:1815:14:42:72:354:490:12:Times:12:Times:F:processes that go beyond the familiar I(0) and I(1) models.
2:34:1874:42:14:72:153:448:12:Times:12:Times:F:Ulrich K. Müller
2:35:1890:14:14:72:198:434:12:Times:12:Times:F:Department of Economics
2:36:1913:14:14:72:172:420:12:Times:12:Times:F:Princeton University
2:37:1933:14:14:72:198:406:12:Times:12:Times:F:Princeton, NJ 08544-1013
2:38:1957:14:28:72:191:392:12:Times:12:Times:F:umueller@princeton.edu
2:39:1979:28:14:72:155:364:12:Times:12:Times:F:Mark W. Watson
2:40:1993:14:14:72:198:350:12:Times:12:Times:F:Department of Economics
2:41:2016:14:14:72:172:336:12:Times:12:Times:F:Princeton University
2:42:2036:14:14:72:198:322:12:Times:12:Times:F:Princeton, NJ 08544-1013
2:43:2060:14:14:72:124:308:12:Times:12:Times:F:and NBER
2:44:2068:14:194:72:192:294:12:Times:12:Times:F:mwatson@princeton.edu
2:45:2089:194:14:72:223:100:12:Times:12:Times:F:A data appendix is available at:
2:46:2121:14:0:72:283:86:12:Times:12:Times:F:http://www.nber.org/data-appendix/w21564
3:47:2161:0:30:72:208:671:301:g_d0_f1:301:g_d0_f1:C:1  Introduction
3:48:2176:30:18:72:540:641:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:This paper discusses inference about trendsin economic time series. By “trend” we mean
3:49:2262:18:18:72:540:623:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:the low-frequency variability evident in a time series after forming moving averages such as
3:50:2354:18:18:72:540:605:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:low-pass (cf. Baxter and King (1999)) or Hodrick and Prescott (1997)filters. To measure
3:51:2441:18:18:72:540:587:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:this low-frequency variability we rely on projections of the series onto a small number of
3:52:2531:18:19:72:540:569:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:trigonometric functions (e.g., discrete Fourier, sine, or cosine transforms). The fact that a
3:53:2624:19:18:72:539:550:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:small number of projection coefficients capture low-frequency variability reflects the scarcity
3:54:2719:18:18:72:539:532:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:of low-frequency information in the data, leading to what is effectively a “small-sample”
3:55:2808:18:18:72:540:514:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:econometric problem. As we show, it is still relatively straightforward to conduct statistical
3:56:2902:18:18:72:540:496:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:inference using the small sample of low-frequency data summaries. Moreover, these low-
3:57:2988:18:18:72:540:478:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:frequency methods are appropriate for both weakly and highly persistent processes. Before
3:58:3077:18:18:72:439:460:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:getting into the details, it is useful tofix ideas by looking at some data.
3:59:3152:18:18:89:539:442:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:Figure 1 plots the value of per-capita GDP growth rates (panel A) and price inflation
3:60:3237:18:18:71:539:424:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:(panel B) for the United States using quarterly data from 1947 through 2014, and where both
3:61:3328:18:18:71:539:406:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:are expressed in percentage points at an annual rate.1The plots show the raw series and two
3:62:3419:18:18:71:539:388:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:“trends”. Thefirst trend was constructed using a band-pass moving averagefilter designed
3:63:3507:18:18:71:540:370:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:to pass cyclical components with periods longer than6≈11years, and the second is the
3:64:3593:18:18:71:539:352:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:full-sample projection of the series onto a constant and twelve cosine functions with periods
3:65:3686:18:18:71:538:334:145:g_d0_f6:65:g_d0_f2:F:2for=112, also designed to capture variability for periods longer than 11 years.2
3:66:3776:18:18:72:539:316:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:Aglanceatthefigure shows that the band-pass and projection trends essentially coincide
3:67:3862:18:18:72:539:298:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:both for GDP, for which there is only moderate trend variability, and inflation, for which
3:68:3952:18:18:71:539:280:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:there is substantial trend variability. This paper focuses on trends computed by projection
3:69:4043:18:19:71:530:262:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:methods because, as we will see, they give rise to simple methods for statistical inference.
3:70:4135:19:17:89:539:243:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:Figure 1 raises several empirical questions. For example, the trend in GDP growth has

5:93:5028:0:252:177:433:664:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:?:Figure 2: Trends in GDP and TFP Growth Rates
5:94:5072:252:18:72:539:412:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:been low for the last decade. Does this portend low average growth over the next decade? Or,
5:95:5164:18:18:72:540:394:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:is there mean reversion in the trend so that the next decade is more likely to exhibit faster
5:96:5257:18:18:72:539:376:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:than average growth? And, whatisthe value of the population mean growth rate of GDP?
5:97:5341:18:18:71:539:358:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:The plot for inflation shows large persistent variation in its trends over the past sixty years.
5:98:5437:18:18:71:539:340:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:Does this suggest that the inflation process is(1)? Or is this behavior consistent with an
5:99:5528:18:18:71:539:322:145:g_d0_f5:145:g_d0_f2:B:(0)process? Alternatively, what about something “between” the(0)and(1)processes?
5:100:5611:18:18:71:539:304:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:More generally, the average value of inflation varies considerably over non-overlapping 10- or
5:101:5705:18:18:71:539:286:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:25-year time periods; average GDP growth rates show less, but still substantial variability.
5:102:5797:18:18:71:539:268:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:How much variability should we expect to see in future 10- and 25-year samples? Section
5:103:5884:18:18:71:539:250:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:3 takes up these questions, along with several other questions that can be answered using
5:104:5973:18:18:71:230:232:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:univariate time series methods.
5:105:6004:18:18:89:539:214:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:Figure 2 plots both total factor productivity (TFP) and GDP growth rates. The trend
5:106:6087:18:18:71:539:196:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:components of the series move together, suggesting (for good reason) that long-run variation
5:107:6179:18:19:71:539:178:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:in GDP and TFP growth rates are closely related. But, exactly how closely are they related?
5:108:6270:19:18:71:539:159:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:And, by how much is the trend growth rate in GDP predicted to increase if the trend growth
5:109:6360:18:18:71:539:141:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:rate of TFP increases by, say, 10 basis points? Section 4 takes up questions like these that
5:110:6452:18:18:71:270:123:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:involve multiple (here two) time series.
5:111:6492:18:0:89:539:105:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:The paper begins, in Section 2, with notation and (finite and large-sample) properties of
6:112:6582:0:18:72:540:671:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:discrete “cosine transforms,” the trigonometric projections used in our analysis.3Sections
6:113:6672:18:18:72:540:653:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:3 and 4 show how these cosine transforms can be used to answer trend-related inference
6:114:6758:18:18:72:540:635:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:questions.  “Low-frequency” variability conjures up spectral analysis, and Section 5 uses
6:115:6847:18:19:72:540:617:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:frequency-domain concepts to explain several facets of the analysis. To keep the focus on
6:116:6936:19:18:72:540:598:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:key ideas and concepts, the analysis in the body of the paper uses relatively simple stochastic
6:117:7031:18:45:72:445:580:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:processes, and Section 6 briefly discusses some extensions and concludes.

9:258:12105:18:18:89:539:273:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:While this discussion has focused on discrete cosine transforms, the representation in (1)
9:259:12195:18:18:71:539:255:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:is seen to hold foranyset of smooth set of weights,Ψ(). Because our interest is focused on
9:260:12286:18:18:71:539:237:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:trends, it is important that the weighted averages extract low-frequency variability from the
9:261:12379:18:18:71:539:219:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:data, and Figure 1 shows that the cosine transforms do just that (also see Section 5.1 below).
9:262:12473:18:18:71:539:201:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:But, low-frequency Fourier or sine transforms are equally well-suited and can be used instead
9:263:12566:18:18:71:500:183:145:g_d0_f2:65:g_d0_f2:B:of the cosine transforms after appropriate modification of the covariance matrixΣ.5
9:264:12649:18:18:85:539:165:49:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:4TheasymptoticcovariancematrixΣis diagonal in both the(0)and(1)models because the cosine
9:265:12739:18:9:72:539:147:100:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:functions are the eigenfunctions of the covariance kernel of a demeaned Wiener process; cf. Phillips (1998).
9:266:12847:9:19:85:540:138:49:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:5For example, while the cosine transforms of an(1)process have the diagonal covariance matrix discussed
9:267:12951:19:15:72:540:119:100:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:in the last paragraph, the covariance matrix of Fourier transforms is somewhat more complicated (cf. Akdi
9:268:13056:15:0:72:158:104:100:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:and Dickey (1998)).
10:269:13076:0:18:89:540:671:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:Andfinally, it is worth stressing that in the asymptotic analysis presented in this section
10:270:13167:18:18:72:539:653:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:the dimension of, which we have denoted by,isheldfixed asgrows large. Such
10:271:13245:18:18:72:540:635:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:asymptotics reflect the notion that there is only a relatively modest amount of information
10:272:13336:18:19:72:540:617:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:about low-frequency phenomena in, say, 65-year realizations of macro time series. In practice,
10:273:13430:19:18:72:540:598:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:this translates into using a relatively small number of low-frequency averages from a long time
10:274:13525:18:18:72:539:580:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:series. Our empirical analysis uses=12from=272quarterly observations because, from
10:275:13609:18:18:71:539:562:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:Figures 1 and 2, this produces a sensible notion of the “trend” in inflation and growth rates
10:276:13702:18:18:71:539:544:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:in GDP and TFP, corresponding the periodicities longer than 11 years. The econometric
10:277:13787:18:18:71:539:526:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:challenge then is to draw valid conclusions from theseobservations. Further discussion of
10:278:13877:18:45:71:287:508:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:thechoiceofmay be found in Section 5.
10:279:13915:45:30:72:464:463:301:g_d0_f1:301:g_d0_f1:C:3   Inference examples: univariate time series
10:280:13961:30:18:72:539:433:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:This section takes upfive examples of statistical inference involving the low-frequency prop-

10:282:14145:18:18:72:540:397:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:the last section yields a simple, and standard, small-sample inference problem involving a
10:283:14235:18:39:72:189:379:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:normal random vector.
10:284:14256:39:26:72:204:340:209:g_d0_f1:209:g_d0_f1:C:3.1  I(0) inference
10:285:14275:26:18:72:539:314:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:Thefirst two examples assume the(0)model and concern inference about the long-run

12:338:17667:39:26:72:386:415:209:g_d0_f1:209:g_d0_f1:C:3.2  Inference about persistence parameters
12:339:17710:26:18:72:539:389:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:As discussed in Section 2, the normal limit forholds under conditions more general
12:340:17794:18:18:72:540:371:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:than the(0)model, and the parameters characterizing the persistence ofdetermine
12:341:17876:18:18:72:539:353:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:the covariance matrixΣ. Thus, inference about these parameters can be conducted using
12:342:17961:18:18:72:540:335:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:methods for inference about the covariance matrix of a multivariate normal random variable.
12:343:18052:18:18:72:450:317:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:This section discusses three classes ofexamples. First a few preliminaries.
12:344:18127:18:18:89:540:299:145:g_d0_f12:145:g_d0_f14:A:Some parametric models of persistence.Section 2 discussed the covariance matrixΣ
12:345:18207:18:18:71:540:281:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:for(0)and(1)processes. Here we describe three other widely used parametric models
12:346:18290:18:18:71:539:263:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:for persistence in economic time series. Thefirst is a sum of(0)and(1)processes: with
12:347:18377:18:19:71:539:245:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:appropriate scaling for the components this is a version of Harvey’s (1989) ’local-level-model’
12:348:18472:19:0:71:195:226:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f6:B:(LLM)=1+()
12:349:18489:0:13:197:213:236:145:g_d0_f10:65:g_d0_f13:?:P
12:350:18491:13:15:210:539:223:65:g_d0_f13:145:g_d0_f2:A:=12where(12)is bivariate(0)with long-run covariance
12:351:18551:15:18:71:539:208:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:matrix22. Another is the ’local-to-unity AR’ model (LTUM) in which=−1+,
12:352:18632:18:18:72:539:190:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:with AR coefficient=1−andan(0)process, as introduced by Cavanagh
12:353:18704:18:18:72:540:172:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:(1985), Chan and Wei (1987) and Phillips (1987). A third is the ’fractional’ model (FRM)
12:354:18792:18:18:72:539:154:145:g_d0_f6:145:g_d0_f2:B:(1−)=whereis(0)with−1232(cf. Baillie (1996) and Robinson (2003)
12:355:18867:18:18:71:539:136:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:for surveys). Each of these processes exhibits different degrees of persistence that depend on
12:356:18961:18:18:71:539:118:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:the value of the model’s parameter value. For example,(0)behavior follows when=0in
12:357:19045:18:0:71:540:100:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:LLM,=0in FRM, andvery large in LTUM. The processes exhibit(1)persistence when

13:359:19203:18:18:71:540:653:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:persistence between(0)and(1),andtheFRMwith0or1allows for persistence
13:360:19277:18:18:71:540:635:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:beyond these extremes. Excluding their(0)and(1)parameterizations, the models yield

13:362:19453:19:18:71:540:598:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:Section 5.2. For now we simply note that this behavior results in different values of the
13:363:19542:18:18:71:182:580:145:g_d0_f2:65:g_d0_f2:B:covariance matrixΣ.6
13:364:19562:18:18:89:539:562:145:g_d0_f12:145:g_d0_f2:A:Scale invariance/equivariance.In many inference problems, interest is focused on the

18:536:29377:19:18:71:539:598:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:density under the null. This yields a simple null hypothesis (that is, a single probability

18:538:29562:18:18:71:539:562:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:the resulting test will control the probability of a false null rejectionon averageover the
18:539:29653:18:18:71:539:544:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f12:B:values ofwithdrawn fromΛ, it won’t necessarily control the rejection probability forall
18:540:29742:18:18:71:539:526:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:values ofallowed under0; that is, the test may not have the correct size. But, because
18:541:29830:18:18:72:539:508:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:any test that controls the null rejection probability forallvalues ofautomatically controls
18:542:29922:18:18:72:539:490:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:the rejection frequencyon averageover, any test with sizeunder0is also a feasible
18:543:30006:18:18:71:539:472:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:sizeunder under0Λ, so it must have power less than or equal to power-maximizing test
18:544:30093:18:18:72:539:454:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:for0Λ. Thus, if a distributionΛcan be found that does control size for all, the resulting
18:545:30185:18:18:71:540:436:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:likelihood ratio test for0Λis guaranteed to be the optimal test for0.SuchaΛis called a
18:546:30274:18:18:71:539:418:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:“least favorable distribution” (LFD). In some problems, least favorable distributions can be
18:547:30366:18:18:71:539:400:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:found by clever reasoning (see Lehmann and Romano (2005) for examples), but this is the
18:548:30453:18:18:71:539:382:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:exception rather than the rule. Numerical methods can alternatively be used to approximate
18:549:30543:18:18:71:539:364:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:the LFD (see Elliott, Müller, and Watson (2015) for discussion and examples), and we will
18:550:30632:18:18:71:206:346:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:utilize that approach here.
18:551:30659:18:19:89:539:328:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:With this background out of the way, we can now return to the problem of determining
18:552:30743:19:18:71:539:309:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:the value of2∆().  We do this allowing for()persistence, and allowto take on
18:553:30825:18:18:71:544:291:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:any value between -0.4 to 1.4. The weighting in the WAP criterion is uniform on,and,
18:554:30910:18:18:71:538:273:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f5:B:conditional on, sets the alternative covariance matrix equal toΓ=Γ()where
18:555:30989:18:18:71:540:255:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:is uniformly distributed on(−55).12Figure 6 summarizes the resulting (pointwise in)

25:819:41849:18:18:89:539:183:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:There are a variety of inference questions that arise in the cointegration model, and several
25:820:41942:18:16:71:538:165:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:of these are straightforward to address using the low-frequency transforms of the data.16For
25:821:42034:16:19:81:539:149:49:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:15To put these values in perspective, recall that the standard neoclassical model, which exhibits balanced
25:822:42140:19:15:72:539:130:100:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:growth, implies that a permanent one percentage point increase in TFP leads to a=(1−)−1percentage
25:823:42239:15:15:71:540:115:100:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:point long-run increase in GDP, wheredenotes the elasticity of output with respect to capital.  Thus
25:824:42340:15:9:71:152:100:100:g_d0_f8:100:g_d0_f2:F:=15if=23.
25:825:42353:9:0:81:293:91:49:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:A:16Also see Bierens (1997) for a related approach.
26:826:42404:0:18:72:544:671:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:example, one question asks whether the cointegrating coefficient takes on a specificvalue,
26:827:42494:18:18:72:539:653:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:that is whether=0. Müller and Watson (2013) take up this question using the low-
26:828:42576:18:18:71:539:635:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:frequency framework. The idea is straightforward. Write−0=−(−0).
26:829:42651:18:19:72:539:617:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:If=0, then the term involvingis absent, but otherwise this component is present.
26:830:42735:19:18:71:547:598:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:Thus,[12(−0)−12]⇒[+0 ]where0=(0−).Thecovariance
26:831:42803:18:18:71:539:580:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:matrix of[(+0)00]0is readily computed and depends on0.  Thus, a hypothesis
26:832:42883:18:18:71:545:562:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:about the value of the cointegrating coefficient, say=0or equivalently=0,can
26:833:42963:18:18:72:540:544:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:be tested as a restriction on the covariance matrix. After imposing invariance restrictions,
26:834:43055:18:18:72:539:526:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:Müller and Watson (2013) show that the scales1and2can be set to unity, so thatis
26:835:43138:18:18:72:539:508:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:the only remaining parameter, and develop an optimal test for=0using a numerical
26:836:43220:18:18:72:540:490:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:approximation to the LFD as discussed in the last section. Thus, as in the other inference
26:837:43310:18:18:72:539:472:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:examples in the low frequency setting, optimal inference about cointegrating coefficients
26:838:43399:18:18:72:518:454:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:becomes a standard problem involving the covariance matrix of normal random vector.
26:839:43482:18:18:89:539:436:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:Perhaps a more interesting question involves the cointegration model’s assumption that
26:840:43568:18:18:72:539:418:145:g_d0_f5:145:g_d0_f2:B:follows an(1)process. What iffollowed another persistent process, perhaps one of the
26:841:43657:18:18:71:539:400:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:parametric processes described in the last section? In the context of “efficient” regression
26:842:43749:18:18:71:539:382:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:inferences about,Elliott(1998)showedthatthe(1)assumption was crucial in the sense
26:843:43832:18:18:71:539:364:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:that large size distortions could arise iffollowed a LTUM instead of an exact(1)process.
26:844:43923:18:18:71:539:346:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:The low-frequency analysis outlined in the last paragraph is not immune from Elliott’s
26:845:44009:18:18:71:539:328:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:critique: it uses the full covariance matrix for[(+0)00]0, and therefore utilizes the
26:846:44099:18:19:71:539:310:145:g_d0_f5:145:g_d0_f2:B:(1)property of(through the(1)covariance matrixthat appears inΣandΣ).
26:847:44176:19:18:72:539:291:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:Müller and Watson (2013) study optimal tests for=0under alternative assumptions
26:848:44257:18:18:71:539:273:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:about the trend process. In particular, they show that if theprocess is unrestricted, then
26:849:44349:18:18:72:539:255:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:the (essentially) most powerful testsimply involves testing whether−0is(0)using
26:850:44434:18:18:71:539:237:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:the LFST test in (4), a solution to the Elliott critique originally proposed by Wright (2000),
26:851:44528:18:18:71:295:219:145:g_d0_f2:65:g_d0_f2:B:although not in the low-frequency setting.17
26:852:44572:18:18:81:539:201:49:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:17The Müller and Watson (2013) result on the near-optimality of the LFST test holds in the cointegration
26:853:44676:18:15:71:539:183:100:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:model with a single cointegrating vector. If there aremultiple cointegrating vectors then it is possible to
26:854:44783:15:0:71:516:168:100:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:obtain more powerful tests even absent assumptionson the process for the common stochastic trends.
27:855:44883:0:228:185:426:664:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:Figure 10: Periodogram of GDP Growth Rates
27:856:44925:228:30:72:384:436:301:g_d0_f1:301:g_d0_f1:C:5  Relationship to Spectral Analysis
27:857:44961:30:18:72:539:406:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:This section relates our method to spectral analysis. Afirst subsection discusses why one
27:858:45050:18:18:72:540:388:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:cannot simply use traditional spectral analysis inference tools to answer questions about
27:859:45139:18:18:72:540:370:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:low-frequency variability. A second subsection derives the limiting covariance matrix of the
27:860:45231:18:18:72:540:352:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:cosine transforms in terms of the spectral density of the underlying time series. A calculation
27:861:45326:18:18:72:540:334:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:shows that the covariance matrix is fully determined by the shape of the spectral density
27:862:45415:18:18:72:539:316:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:close to the origin, a function we call the “local-to-zero spectrum.”18In the third subsection,
27:863:45510:18:18:71:539:298:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:we present a central limit theorem for low frequency transforms under general forms of the
27:864:45600:18:39:71:188:280:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:local-to-zero spectrum.
27:865:45623:39:23:72:540:241:209:g_d0_f1:209:g_d0_f1:F:5.1  Scarcity of low-frequency information and its extraction using
27:866:45690:23:26:108:231:218:209:g_d0_f1:209:g_d0_f1:C:cosine transforms
27:867:45707:26:18:72:539:192:145:g_d0_f12:145:g_d0_f2:B:Scarcity of low-frequency information.In traditional spectral analysis (see, for instance,
27:868:45797:18:18:72:540:174:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:Priestley (1981) or Brockwell and Davis (1991)), the periodogram is the starting point to
27:869:45886:18:18:72:539:156:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:learn about the spectral properties of a timeseries. Figure 10 plots the periodogram for
27:870:45974:18:18:72:539:138:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:quarterly GDP growth rates. The shaded portion of thefigure shows the frequencies corre-
27:871:46062:18:18:72:540:120:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:sponding to periods of 11 years or longer. A mere six periodogram ordinates fall into this
27:872:46152:18:0:81:429:102:49:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:A:18This subsection largely follows the development in Müller and Watson (2015).
28:873:46232:0:30:117:494:663:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:Figure 11:2Plot of Generic Trigonometric Series on Cosine Transforms
28:874:46301:30:112:91:531:633:121:g_d0_f2:121:g_d0_f2:A:average over phase shiftmaximum over phase shiftminimum over phase shift
28:875:46373:112:46:130:488:521:36:g_d0_f2:36:g_d0_f21:?:multiples ofmultiples ofmultiples of
28:876:46412:46:18:72:539:475:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:low-frequency region, and this count would remain unaltered if GDP growth was instead
28:877:46497:18:18:72:540:457:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:sampled at the monthly or even weekly frequency. Intuitively, 67 years of data contain only
28:878:46588:18:18:72:448:439:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:limited information about components with periods of 11 years or longer.
28:879:46660:18:18:89:539:421:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:Traditional inference about the shape and value of the spectral density is based on averag-
28:880:46751:18:18:72:540:403:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:ing periodogram ordinates. The asymptotics are driven by an assumption that the spectrum
28:881:46839:18:19:72:540:385:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:is locallyflat (that is, the spectrum isfixed and continuous), so that as the sample size grows,
28:882:46935:19:18:72:540:366:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:eventually there will be more and more relevant periodogram ordinates to estimate the value
28:883:47026:18:18:72:540:348:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:of the spectral density at any given point. Under such asymptotics, Laws of Large Numbers
28:884:47115:18:18:72:523:330:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:and Central Limit Theorems are applicable and enable asymptotically normal inference.
28:885:47200:18:18:89:539:312:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:But with the relevant number of periodogram ordinates as small as six, such asymptotics
28:886:47287:18:18:72:540:294:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:do not provide good approximations. What is more, in any non-(0)model, the spectrum
28:887:47371:18:18:72:540:276:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:has non-negligible curvature even within a(−1)band around zero, and this band contains
28:888:47459:18:18:72:539:258:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:afixed number of periodogram ordinates. For these reasons, the traditional inference tools
28:889:47549:18:18:72:311:240:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:of spectral analysis are not directly applicable.
28:890:47598:18:18:89:539:222:145:g_d0_f12:145:g_d0_f2:A:Extracting low-frequency information using cosine transforms.Do a small number of
28:891:47679:18:18:72:539:204:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:cosine transforms do a good job at extracting the low-frequency information thatiscontained
28:892:47770:18:18:72:540:186:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:in the sample data? One useful way to answer this question is to consider the a perfectly
28:893:47859:18:18:72:545:168:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:periodic time series of frequency,=sin(+)with phase shift∈[0].Ideally,the
28:894:47941:18:18:71:539:150:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f5:B:low-frequency cosine transformswould capture the entire variation offor0≤≤ ̄
28:895:48023:18:18:71:539:132:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:for small values of ̄and none of the variation if ̄. In large samples, with ̄= ̄for
28:896:48114:18:18:71:539:114:145:g_d0_f3:145:g_d0_f2:B:fixed ̄, the corresponding2of a regression ofonΨis well approximated by the2of
28:897:48199:18:0:71:539:96:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:a continuous time regression ofsin(+)on the×1cosine functionsΨ()and a constant
29:898:48284:0:18:72:545:671:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:for∈[01]. Figure 11 plots this continuous time2as a function offor ̄=12.While
29:899:48367:18:18:72:539:653:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:the2plot does not follow the ideal step function form, it still provides evidence that the
29:900:48458:18:18:72:539:635:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:low-frequency cosine transforms do a reasonably good job at extracting the low-frequency
29:901:48546:18:39:72:198:617:145:g_d0_f2:65:g_d0_f2:B:information of interest.19
29:902:48572:39:26:72:262:578:209:g_d0_f1:209:g_d0_f1:C:5.2  Local-to-Zero Spectra
29:903:48598:26:18:72:540:552:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:BecauseΣis the limiting covariance matrix of the cosine transforms, which in turn are
29:904:48683:18:18:72:539:534:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:weighted averages of the underlying series, the elements ofΣdepend on the autocovari-
29:905:48770:18:18:72:540:516:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:ances of the-process. These, in turn, are linked to spectrum of the process, and this makes
29:906:48862:18:18:72:539:498:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:it possible to expressΣas a function of the spectrum. Because the cosine transforms extract
29:907:48953:18:18:72:539:480:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:low-frequency information in, it should not be surprising that it is the spectrum close to
29:908:49044:18:18:72:539:462:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:zero that determinesΣ. The remainder of this subsection makes this explicit by showing
29:909:49130:18:18:72:540:444:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f6:B:thatΣcan expressed as a function of the spectrum evaluated at frequencies in a(−1)
29:910:49214:18:18:71:470:426:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:neighborhood of frequency zero, a function we call the local-to-zero spectrum.
29:911:49292:18:18:89:539:408:145:g_d0_f12:145:g_d0_f2:A:Local-to-zero spectrum for a stationary process.Suppose thatis a stationary process
29:912:49377:18:18:71:539:390:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:with spectral density().20Suppose that in the(−1)neighborhood of the origin, the
29:913:49462:18:25:71:369:372:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:suitably scaled spectral density converges (in an1sense)
29:914:49519:25:25:247:539:347:145:g_d0_f5:145:g_d0_f2:A:1−2()→()(5)
29:915:49540:25:18:72:539:322:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:For example, the FRM is traditionally defined by the assumption that()is proportional
29:916:49628:18:18:72:539:304:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:to||−2for small,sothat(5)holdswith=12−. More generally, the function()is
29:917:49707:18:18:71:539:286:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:the large sample limit of the shape of the original spectrumclose to the origin, and we
29:918:49796:18:18:71:372:268:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:correspondingly refer to it as the “local-to-zero” spectrum.
29:919:49856:18:31:89:288:250:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:Now consider a weighted average of,

36:1348:60683:0:0:409:539:93:65:g_d0_f19:145:g_d0_f7:F:−−1=−12−1(1−
37:1349:60703:0:18:72:539:671:145:g_d0_f6:145:g_d0_f2:B:()−)→0.  For instance, even integrable poles inat frequencies other than zero
37:1350:60785:18:18:72:189:653:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:can be accommodated.
37:1351:60805:18:18:89:539:635:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f7:A:Third, supposeis an AR(1) process with local-to-unity coefficient=1−
37:1352:60879:18:19:71:556:617:145:g_d0_f5:145:g_d0_f2:B:and innovation variance equal to−1.Then=−1,≥0.Thus
37:1353:60942:19:0:72:91:598:145:g_d0_f5:65:g_d0_f15:C:−1

37:1356:60953:0:10:140:146:608:145:g_d0_f10:145:g_d0_f10:C:¡

37:1358:60969:0:10:218:228:608:145:g_d0_f10:65:g_d0_f15:?:¢2
37:1359:60971:10:0:232:244:598:145:g_d0_f7:145:g_d0_f7:C:→
37:1360:60972:0:14:247:264:608:145:g_d0_f10:65:g_d0_f19:A:R∞
37:1361:60974:14:0:253:271:594:65:g_d0_f13:145:g_d0_f5:A:
37:1362:60976:0:23:271:542:603:65:g_d0_f19:145:g_d0_f2:A:−2,whichcanbemadearbitrarilysmallbychoos-
37:1363:61021:23:18:72:540:580:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:inglarge. Further,()=(2)−1−2|1−−i|2, which is seen to satisfy (10) with
37:1364:61103:18:18:72:539:562:145:g_d0_f5:145:g_d0_f2:B:()=(2)−1(2+2)−1.  Conditions (11) and (12) also hold in this example, since
37:1365:61183:18:18:71:152:544:145:g_d0_f5:145:g_d0_f2:B:()≤(2)−1.
37:1366:61196:18:18:89:539:526:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:Corresponding central limit theorems for a vector of weighted averages of one or multiple
37:1367:61285:18:45:71:479:508:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:time series follow readily from Theorem 1 by invoking the Cramer-Wold device.
37:1368:61362:45:30:72:271:463:301:g_d0_f1:301:g_d0_f1:C:6  Concluding remarks
37:1369:61383:30:18:72:540:433:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:Inference about low-frequency phenomena is challenging because of the scarcity of corre-
37:1370:61471:18:18:72:539:415:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f5:B:sponding sampling information.  We suggest extracting this information by computing
37:1371:61555:18:18:72:540:397:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:trigonometrically weighted averages of the time series of interest.  We then apply an as-
37:1372:61644:18:18:72:539:379:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:ymptotic framework that explicitly accounts for the scarcity by keeping the numberfixed,
37:1373:61733:18:18:71:539:361:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f5:B:so that in the limit, the inference problem becomes a small sample problem involving
37:1374:61818:18:19:71:539:343:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:Gaussian variables. In many instances, this small sample problem is readily solved by classic
37:1375:61911:19:18:71:539:324:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:results about inference in small Gaussian samples. In other cases, one can apply analytical
37:1376:62002:18:18:71:449:306:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:or numerical approaches to derive powerful inference fromfirst principles.
37:1377:62076:18:18:89:539:288:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:The results presented here did not allow for a deterministic time trend in theprocess.
37:1378:62164:18:18:71:539:270:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:One approach to dealing with deterministic trends is to use inference methods that are
37:1379:62250:18:18:71:539:252:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:unaffected by their presence. Alternatively, for example in the context of measuring the
37:1380:62338:18:18:71:539:234:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:covariability of multiple time series, one might model the presence of common time trends.
37:1381:62428:18:18:71:539:216:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:In either case, it is straightforward to isolate the deterministic trend component by using a
37:1382:62521:18:18:71:539:198:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:suitable set ofweight functions, where one is equal to the (demeaned) trend (and whose
37:1383:62608:18:18:71:539:180:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:value is potentially ignored in the subsequent analysis), and the other−1trigonometric
37:1384:62695:18:18:71:539:162:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:weights are orthogonal to both a constant and a time trend. One such set of weights is
37:1385:62781:18:18:71:264:144:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:derived in Müller and Watson (2008).
37:1386:62817:18:18:89:539:126:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f5:A:The results presented here were also based on the large sample Gaussianity of the
37:1387:62899:18:18:72:540:108:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:weighted averages due to a central limit theorem. The key mechanisms of the approach,
37:1388:62984:18:0:72:540:90:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:however, can be used to deduce a non-Gaussian limit distribution after suitably adjusting
38:1389:63075:0:18:72:540:671:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:the solution of the now non-Gaussian limit problem.  For example, consider a stochastic
38:1390:63162:18:18:72:539:653:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:volatility model for the scalar series, where the volatility process is, say, local-to-unity.
38:1391:63257:18:18:72:540:635:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:Conditional on the volatility path, the cosine transforms have a Gaussian limit, with a
38:1392:63344:18:19:72:540:617:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:covariance matrix that depends on the realization of the volatility path. The unconditional
38:1393:63435:19:18:72:540:598:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:distribution thus becomes a mixture of normals, and the corresponding small sample problem
38:1394:63525:18:18:72:401:580:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:becomes inference about the parameters of this normal mixture.
38:1395:63587:18:18:89:539:562:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:More generally, we believe our general approach to low-frequency econometrics to be
38:1396:63670:18:18:72:540:544:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:useful for issues beyond those discussed here. For instance, in Müller and Watson (2015),
38:1397:63759:18:18:72:540:526:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:we use this framework to derive predictive setsfor very long-run predictions that are valid
38:1398:63850:18:18:72:540:508:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:for a wide range of persistent processes. We are currently working on inference about the
38:1399:63939:18:18:72:539:490:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:degree of covariability of potentially non-(0)series. And it would also be of great interest
38:1400:64032:18:18:71:539:472:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:to connect the low-frequency econometrics approach more directly with structural economic
38:1401:64121:18:38:71:426:454:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:models, such as asset pricing models that stress long-run uncertainty.
